# VIDEO 4 — THE RESEARCH BEHIND EVERY FAMILY LAW FIRM THAT GETS GREAT REFERRALS

```
VIDEO 4 — The Research Behind Every Family Law Firm That Gets Great Referrals
Mirror Post: Day 6 — "Most family law marketing starts from the wrong place... None of it started with the actual question: Why does a person in your market choose one family law attorney over another?"
Stage: B3/E3
Pillar: Insider View
Target Length: 10–12 min
Dopamine Driver: Insider Knowledge + Pattern Recognition
Thumbnail Angle: Split screen — cluttered generic content calendar on the left, a clean decision-stage map with LGD branding on the right; Roger pointing at the right side with the text "THEY NEVER RESEARCHED THIS"
```

---

## SECTION 1 — HOOK (0:00–0:45)

**Primary Hook:**

If I showed you exactly why clients in your market choose one family law attorney over another — not in general, but the specific decision sequence for the specific case type you want more of — would that change how you market your firm? Because that research exists. Most attorneys have never seen it. And almost none of their marketing agencies have done it.

[B-ROLL: Close-up of a hand scrolling through a generic law firm website — stock photos, broad practice area pages, no specificity]

**Alt Hook A (for returning subscribers):**

Last week we talked about the pipeline gap — the difference between firms that are waiting for referrals and firms that are generating them deliberately. This week I want to show you the research layer underneath that. Because before you produce a single piece of content, before you write one LinkedIn post or record one video, there's a map you need to build. And most attorneys — and most marketing agencies — have never built it.

**Alt Hook B (pattern-interrupt / data-led):**

Here's what I've found after working with family law firms across dozens of markets: every firm that has a genuinely strong referral pipeline has one thing in common. It's not the best website. It's not the most content. It's not even the highest-rated reviews. It's that someone — at some point — sat down and actually mapped how their ideal client makes a decision. And then built everything around that map.

**The Promise:**

I'm Roger with Legal Growth Dynamics. Before we produce a single piece of content for any family law firm, we map the decision sequence for their ideal client. This video walks through what that map looks like — and why it changes everything downstream.

---

## SECTION 2 — SETUP / PROBLEM FRAME (0:45–2:00)

Here's where most family law marketing goes wrong — and I mean structurally wrong, not just tactically wrong.

An attorney or their marketing agency decides they need more cases. So they start producing content. Blog posts about "what to expect in a divorce." Videos about "how custody works in [state]." Social media posts about recent case outcomes. Maybe they run some ads. And for a while it feels like progress — there's activity, there's output, there's something to point to.

But the content doesn't convert the way they expected. The phone rings, but not from the kind of clients they wanted. Or the phone doesn't ring at all. And eventually they conclude one of two things: either content marketing doesn't work for family law, or they need a different agency.

[B-ROLL: Time-lapse of someone typing at a laptop, scrolling through content drafts, uploading to a website — lots of motion, no apparent result]

What they almost never conclude — because no one tells them — is that the content was never mapped to how their ideal client actually makes a decision. It wasn't wrong content in terms of quality. It was wrong content in terms of timing. It was speaking to people at the wrong stage of their decision process.

Think about what actually happens when someone realizes they might be heading toward a divorce. They don't immediately search for attorneys. Most people don't. The first thing they do is try to understand their situation. They search for what divorce actually costs. They search for how courts handle custody in their state. They search for whether their spouse can take the house. They spend weeks — sometimes months — in a research phase before they ever think about who to call.

[B-ROLL: Person late at night on a laptop, multiple browser tabs open, looking overwhelmed — conveys the private research phase]

If your content is only targeting people who are already ready to hire — if every piece you produce is essentially "here's why you should call us" — you're missing the majority of the decision process. You're only showing up at the end of a journey that started long before they were ready to hear your pitch.

That's the problem. And it's not a content problem. It's a research problem. The research wasn't done before the content was produced.

---

## SECTION 3 — THE TURN (2:00–3:30)

Here's the reframe I want to offer you — and it's one that changes the entire approach.

Marketing for a family law firm is not primarily about convincing people to hire you. It's about being present at every stage of the decision journey they're already on. The decision to hire a family law attorney is almost never an impulse. It's almost always the end of a research process. Your job is to be the most credible, most helpful, most specific source of intelligence at every stage of that process — so that when they are ready to call someone, you're the name they've come to trust.

[B-ROLL: Diagram animation — a linear journey from "problem awareness" through research phases to "attorney selection," with LGD-style visual branding]

That's a completely different mandate than most agencies give their clients. Most agencies are focused on the conversion stage — the part where someone is ready to call. And that's a meaningful stage. But if you've been absent from the earlier stages, you're competing with the attorney who wasn't.

This is what National Library Week, in a strange way, captures perfectly. The library isn't where you go when you've already decided what book you want. It's where you go to research. To explore. To understand something you don't yet fully understand. Your content needs to be that library — specifically for the person who is trying to understand what they're facing before they're ready to ask for help.

[B-ROLL: Empty library with sunlight through tall windows — conveying quiet, authoritative intelligence rather than hustle]

The family law firms that consistently produce great referrals — and great organic intake — are the ones whose content covers the full library. Not just the checkout desk.

---

## SECTION 4 — THE PROOF / MECHANISM (3:30–7:00)

[CHAPTER: The Decision Sequence — What It Is and Why It Matters (~3:30)]

Let me walk you through the actual framework. We call it the Decision Sequence Map, and here's what it covers.

Every potential family law client goes through six distinct stages between the moment they first recognize a problem and the moment they sign a retainer. The stages are: Problem Awareness, Situation Research, Options Discovery, Advisor Evaluation, Commitment Readiness, and Selection.

At Stage One — Problem Awareness — they know something is wrong in their life but they don't yet have a legal frame for it. They're not searching for attorneys. They're searching for "signs of financial abuse in marriage" or "how to tell if a divorce is inevitable" or "what happens to kids when parents separate." Content at this stage needs to be empathetic, educational, and non-promotional. If you're running ads at Stage One people, you're burning money. If you have content that speaks to Stage One, you're building an audience before they know they need you.

[B-ROLL: Person reading an article on their phone, expression shifting from casual to concerned — conveying the moment of problem recognition]

At Stage Three — Options Discovery — they're starting to understand that an attorney is probably involved in their future. But they're still not evaluating specific attorneys. They're evaluating options. Mediation versus litigation. Collaborative divorce versus traditional. Contested versus uncontested. Your content at this stage positions you as the expert who can help them understand the landscape — not sell them a service.

[PACING NOTE: Slow slightly here — this is the section where information density is highest. Pause between stage descriptions. Let each stage land before moving to the next.]

At Stage Five — Commitment Readiness — they're finally ready to talk to someone. This is where most law firm marketing lives. This is also where the competition is fiercest, because every attorney in your market is targeting this stage. If this is your only content territory, you're in a crowded room.

[B-ROLL: Split-screen — on one side, a generic law firm website homepage with "Call for a Free Consultation"; on the other, a multi-part educational video series with specific stage-targeted content]

Now here's the part that matters most for the specific case types you want to attract. The sequence isn't identical across case types. A high-asset divorce has a different sequence than a custody modification. A business owner going through a divorce is in a completely different research pattern than a stay-at-home parent facing a separation. The content that converts a high-asset client is not the same content that converts a custody modification client — not because the quality is different, but because the decision journey is different.

[CHAPTER: How to Research Your Own Market's Decision Sequence (~5:00)]

Now let me give you the practical layer. How do you actually map your own market's decision sequence?

The first source is your own intake data. If your intake team is doing their job — and most aren't doing this part — they're capturing not just where the client came from, but what triggered the call. Not "how did you hear about us," which gives you referral source. What I mean is: what made you call us today, specifically? What were you looking for when you found us? What was the tipping point? That question, asked consistently, produces gold. It tells you what stage your clients are actually converting from. And in most cases, when attorneys run this data, they find they're converting almost entirely from Stage Five and Six — which means they have an enormous untouched market at Stages One through Four.

[B-ROLL: Intake form on a screen with a highlighted field — "What made you reach out today?"]

[PACING NOTE: Pick up pace slightly here — this section is more actionable and should feel energizing, not laborious.]

The second source is your best clients. Not a survey — a conversation. The question I recommend is this: "When you first realized you might need to speak with someone about your situation — not that you'd hired us, but the moment you first thought you might need an attorney — what were you thinking about?" That one question reveals the Stage One or Stage Two moment. And it tells you the exact emotional context your content needs to mirror to reach that person before they're in the market.

[B-ROLL: Two people in a relaxed post-engagement follow-up meeting — not a formal deposition setting, but a casual debrief]

The third source is competitor content analysis. Look at the top-performing content from family law firms in comparable markets — not your direct competitors, because you want to see what's working without giving them your attention. Look at which pieces get the most engagement, the most comments, the most shares. Almost always, those pieces are Stage Two or Three content. The research content. The explainer content. The "here's what this actually means for your situation" content. Because that's what people share when they're trying to understand something — not ads.

[CHAPTER: How to Use the Map Once You Have It (~6:15)]

Once you have the map, the calendar builds itself. This is the part that surprises most attorneys I work with.

When you know the six stages, and you know where your ideal clients convert, and you know which case types have which sequences, you don't have to guess what to post this week. You know exactly what stage needs content. You know what format serves that stage best — because educational Stage Two content doesn't need to be a call to action, it needs to be genuinely useful. And you know what a meaningful 90-day content block looks like for each case type you want to attract more of.

The calendar that emerges from a real decision sequence map is completely different from a generic content calendar. A generic calendar is organized around topics. A map-driven calendar is organized around stages. And the difference in conversion is significant — because you're not just producing volume, you're producing coverage. You're covering the full journey instead of camping at the conversion moment.

[B-ROLL: Side-by-side of two content calendars — one organized by topic headers (generic), one organized by stage labels and case type (specific)]

---

## SECTION 5 — THE CLOSE (7:00–END)

**5A — Identity Anchor:**

If you're a managing partner who's been burned by agencies that promised content results and delivered activity without impact — this is why. It wasn't the content quality. It was the absence of a map. Good content without a decision sequence map is a library with books shelved randomly. The resources are there. The access isn't.

The firms that build referral pipelines consistently — not occasionally, not after a lucky Google review spike, but consistently — are the firms whose content is organized around how their clients think, not around what's convenient to produce.

That's the research most attorneys haven't done. And it's the research we do first, before we produce a single asset.

**5B — CTA:**

If you want to see what the decision sequence framework actually looks like — the one we use before we start any family law engagement — DM me the word SEQUENCE. One word. I'll send you the framework directly. No call required, no pitch. Just the actual tool we use so you can see whether it changes how you're thinking about your content.

DM me SEQUENCE.

**5C — Channel Hook:**

Tomorrow we're going to go clinical. I'm going to walk you through the four vital signs of a healthy family law pipeline — and the one that's almost always the problem when a managing partner tells me their growth has stalled. If you've ever wondered whether your pipeline is structurally sound or just temporarily okay, you'll want to watch that one.

## VALUE BOMB VARIANT — VIDEO 4

**Title:** The 6 Stages Every Family Law Client Goes Through Before They Call You

Most family law content is aimed at Stage Five or Stage Six — the point where someone is ready to hire. That's the smallest part of the decision journey, and the most competitive.

Here's what actually happens from the beginning.

Stage One is Problem Awareness. They know something is wrong but they don't have a legal frame for it. They're searching for emotional context, not attorneys. If your content isn't there, someone else's is.

Stage Two is Situation Research. They're trying to understand what their situation means legally. What does equitable distribution actually mean in their state? What are the real factors in a custody determination? This is where educational content wins — not because it sells, but because it builds trust before they're ready to be sold to.

Stage Three is Options Discovery. Mediation or litigation. Contested or uncontested. Collaborative or traditional. If you're not the one explaining the landscape, you're not the one they come back to when they're ready to choose.

Stage Four is Advisor Evaluation. This is the comparison phase. They've narrowed the field. They're watching your videos, reading your posts, checking your reviews, gauging whether you seem like someone they can trust with something this hard. What they find in the next 20 minutes either builds or breaks your candidacy.

Stage Five is Commitment Readiness. They've decided to call someone. This is where most law firm content lives — and where the competition is fiercest, because it's the stage everyone targets.

Stage Six is Selection. They're choosing between two or three finalists. At this point, your content from Stages One through Four is either working in your favor or it's absent — and absent means disadvantage.

Map your content to all six stages. Identify which stages you're missing. Start there.

DM me SEQUENCE and I'll send you the framework.

---

## CONTRARIAN VARIANT — VIDEO 4

**Title:** Why Researching Your Ideal Client Is a Waste of Time (And What to Do Instead)

Every marketing consultant will tell you to research your ideal client. Build a persona. Know their pain points. Understand their demographics.

I'm going to tell you that's mostly a waste of time for family law — and here's why.

Personas are static. They describe who your ideal client is. They don't describe what your ideal client does when they're in the middle of the most stressful situation of their life and trying to figure out whether they need a lawyer and who that lawyer should be.

The research that actually matters isn't about who they are. It's about what they do — in sequence — between the moment they first sense a problem and the moment they sign a retainer with someone.

That's the research most attorneys have never done. And it's the research that makes the difference between content that converts and content that produces nothing except evidence that you're posting consistently.

What does this research look like? It starts with your best past clients. Not a survey. A conversation. One question: "When did you first realize you were going to need an attorney — not that you'd chosen us, but the first moment the thought occurred to you?" What you'll hear is Stage One. The private moment. The late-night Google search. The friend's offhand comment. The financial document that didn't add up.

That moment is where your content needs to start. Not "call us for a consultation." Not "we handle all family law matters." The moment when they first knew something was wrong and didn't know what to do about it yet.

Find that moment in your market. Build content that meets people there. Everything else follows.

DM me SEQUENCE. I'll send you the framework.

---

# VIDEO 5 — THE 4 VITAL SIGNS OF A HEALTHY LAW FIRM PIPELINE

```
VIDEO 5 — The 4 Vital Signs of a Healthy Law Firm Pipeline
Mirror Post: Day 7 — "Most attorneys run annual physicals. Almost none run them for their firm... here are the four vital signs I check when a managing partner tells me their pipeline 'isn't where they want it to be.'"
Stage: B3/E3
Pillar: Practical Tool
Target Length: 10–12 min
Dopamine Driver: Self-Assessment + Revelation
Thumbnail Angle: Roger in business casual looking directly at camera, holding a clipboard or tablet; large text overlay "YOUR PIPELINE IS SICK" with a red underline, and smaller text below "Here's how to tell"
```

---

## SECTION 1 — HOOK (0:00–0:45)

**Primary Hook:**

If you went to the doctor and they only checked your blood pressure — and ignored your cholesterol, your weight, your glucose levels, everything else — you'd call that a bad physical. But that's exactly how most family law firms evaluate their pipeline health. They look at revenue. Quarterly. And they call it a review. Today I'm going to walk you through the four vital signs that actually tell you whether your pipeline is healthy — and which one is most likely to be the reason you're not growing the way you want to be.

[B-ROLL: Split screen — doctor's office chart with multiple data points on one side; a simple revenue spreadsheet with one column on the other]

**Alt Hook A (for returning subscribers):**

Yesterday we talked about the decision sequence — the map that should drive every content decision your firm makes. Today I want to go one level up. Before the content strategy, before the marketing plan, you need to know where your pipeline is actually sick. Because the treatment depends on the diagnosis. And most managing partners are treating symptoms without knowing what's causing them.

**Alt Hook B (pattern-interrupt / contrarian):**

Most pipeline problems in family law aren't revenue problems. Revenue is a lagging indicator — it tells you what already happened. The real problems show up in four places, and most of them are invisible until they become urgent. By the time revenue drops, two or three of these vital signs have been deteriorating for months.

**The Promise:**

I'm Roger with Legal Growth Dynamics. We work specifically with family law managing partners, and these four vital signs come directly from the diagnostic we run before we start any engagement. They're what we found actually predicts pipeline health — not revenue alone.

---

## SECTION 2 — SETUP / PROBLEM FRAME (0:45–2:00)

Here's what a managing partner usually tells me when they first reach out.

"My revenue is okay but it's not growing. The referrals are inconsistent. I feel like I'm working harder than I should be for the caseload I have. And I don't have a clear sense of whether next quarter is going to look like this quarter or something worse."

That's the symptom description. And it's accurate — but it's incomplete. Because there are at least four different things that could be causing those symptoms, and the right fix for one is completely different from the right fix for another.

[B-ROLL: Managing partner at a desk, looking at a laptop, expression conveying low-grade concern — not panic, but the look of someone who knows something's off but can't name it]

If the problem is source concentration — too much revenue dependent on too few referral sources — the answer is diversification. But if the problem is intake speed — qualified prospects calling and not converting because follow-up is slow — the answer is operational, not marketing. And if the problem is case quality — they're getting cases but the wrong cases — the answer is positioning. Producing more content or running more ads won't fix an intake speed problem. And fixing intake speed won't fix a positioning problem.

This is why the diagnosis has to precede the treatment. And why looking only at revenue tells you almost nothing useful about which problem you actually have.

[B-ROLL: Medical diagnostic chart showing multiple indicators — not a single metric but a whole panel — conveying the completeness of a proper health check]

Most attorneys I talk to have never done this diagnostic. They've never sat down and looked at their pipeline through all four of these lenses at the same time. Which means they're making marketing decisions — significant financial decisions — based on incomplete information.

That changes today. Let's go through each vital sign.

---

## SECTION 3 — THE TURN (2:00–3:30)

Here's the shift I want you to make before we go through the framework.

Stop thinking about your pipeline as a revenue number and start thinking about it as a system with multiple interdependent components. A system that, like any biological system, can be partially healthy and partially compromised at the same time. And where one compromised component can mask the health of others — right up until it doesn't.

[B-ROLL: Simple animated diagram of a pipeline system with four labeled sections, each with a status indicator — some green, some yellow, one red]

A firm that has strong referral sources but a broken intake process looks fine on the outside — until a competitor starts showing up in the referral conversations those sources are having. A firm that has great content and great intake but is over-dependent on one referral relationship looks diversified — until that relationship retires or changes firms.

The four vital signs I'm going to walk you through are designed to surface the hidden vulnerabilities. The ones that feel fine right now but are actually the reasons you're not at the growth trajectory you want to be.

[B-ROLL: Calm water surface with a slight current visible just below — conveying that the surface looks fine but there's movement underneath]

Every one of these vital signs is measurable. Not estimated — actually measurable with data you already have. That's important, because I'm not going to give you a framework that requires you to guess. I'm going to give you a framework that requires you to look.

---

## SECTION 4 — THE PROOF / MECHANISM (3:30–7:00)

[CHAPTER: Vital Sign 1 — Source Concentration Risk (~3:30)]

The first vital sign is source concentration — specifically, how dependent your pipeline is on a small number of referral sources or a single channel.

Here's the rule we use: if more than 70% of your new case revenue comes from fewer than three sources — or from a single channel like referrals in general — you have a concentration problem. Not a crisis, not necessarily. But a structural vulnerability that limits both your growth ceiling and your stability.

[B-ROLL: A pie chart with one enormous slice labeled "Referrals" and tiny slivers for everything else — visually conveying the imbalance]

Here's why this matters in practice. If you're a six-attorney family law firm and 70% of your revenue comes from attorney referrals — which is extremely common — and two of your four major referral relationships retire, change firms, or shift their referral habits over the next 18 months, you're not at 70%. You're potentially at 30% or 40% of what you had. And you have no alternative channel that's been built up to absorb that.

The referral audit I recommend takes about 30 minutes and requires one thing: a spreadsheet of every matter opened in the last 24 months with the intake source. Sort by source. Calculate what percentage of revenue by matter value came from each source and each source type. That's your concentration map.

[B-ROLL: Simple spreadsheet on screen — columns: matter, intake source, case type, revenue — being reviewed by someone off-camera]

[PACING NOTE: This is a denser analytical section. Keep delivery steady and concrete. Specific numbers and actions are the retention anchor here.]

What does healthy look like? By firm stage, it varies. A solo practitioner who's been in the market for three years can sustain a higher referral concentration than a four-attorney firm trying to scale. But at every stage, the goal is movement toward a world where no single source represents more than 25% of your intake and at least one channel — content, digital, or relationship-based — is growing rather than static.

[CHAPTER: Vital Signs 2 and 3 — Speed and Case Quality (~5:00)]

Vital Sign Two is intake speed — specifically, the gap between a prospect's first contact and the first substantive conversation with someone at your firm.

The data on this is unambiguous. A family law prospect who doesn't hear back within the first two to four hours of their first contact converts at a dramatically lower rate than one who does. In some studies, the conversion rate difference between a 30-minute response and a 24-hour response is greater than 50%. That's not a small margin. That's the difference between winning and losing half the consultations you could otherwise have.

[B-ROLL: Phone showing a missed call notification and a subsequent text from a different attorney's firm — conveying the competitive cost of slow response]

Run this diagnostic: pull your last 30 consultation requests — call, form submission, email, any channel — and measure the time between first contact and first response. Not first appointment — first response. If that average is more than four hours on business days, you have a Vital Sign Two problem. And no amount of better content is going to fix a pipeline that's leaking consultations after they've already arrived.

Vital Sign Three is revenue per matter — or more precisely, whether the cases you're winning reflect the positioning you want. This is a positioning metric masquerading as a financial metric.

[B-ROLL: Simple bar chart showing revenue per matter by case type — some bars tall, some very short — conveying the distribution]

If your average high-asset divorce matter produces five to six times the revenue of your average uncontested divorce, but 60% of your new matters are uncontested divorces — you have a case quality problem. Not because uncontested divorces are bad business, but because your pipeline is not reflecting your positioning. And if your content, your referral relationships, and your online presence don't specifically signal that you're the expert for high-asset matters, you'll keep attracting the cases that are easiest to explain rather than the cases that build your business.

[PACING NOTE: Brief pause before Vital Sign 4 — let the revenue per matter point land. This is often the one that surprises managing partners most.]

[CHAPTER: Vital Sign 4 — The Missing Channel (~6:20)]

Vital Sign Four is the one that gets the most pushback — and the one that, in my experience, is the most predictive of whether a firm will be in a stronger or weaker position 18 months from now.

Content-sourced intake. Specifically, what percentage of your new matters came from someone who found you through content you produced — a video, an article, a social post — before they were referred by anyone or connected with you through any paid channel.

For most family law firms, this number is zero. Or close enough to zero to be the same thing.

[B-ROLL: Website analytics screen showing traffic sources — one tiny sliver labeled "Organic/Content" compared to large portions from direct and referral]

I want to be clear about why this matters. It's not because content-sourced clients are necessarily better than referred clients. It's because content-sourced intake is the only channel that compounds. Referral relationships depend on the continued goodwill and activity of individual humans — which is inherently fragile. Content that exists and accumulates authority over time doesn't require a referral source to stay healthy, stay local, or stay in practice.

The type of case that content-sourced clients typically bring is also worth noting. Because content-sourced prospects have usually done more research before they call, they tend to arrive at the consultation with higher baseline understanding of their situation, more realistic expectations, and greater readiness to engage. That's a different intake experience than the prospect who calls because a friend said to call — who may arrive with significantly less context and more emotional volatility to manage.

If your content-sourced intake is zero — if no one in the last 12 months found you because of something you produced and published — that's the vital sign most likely to limit your growth ceiling. Because you're 100% dependent on channels you don't control.

[B-ROLL: Two intake consultation scenarios side by side — one prospect arrivingemotionally distressed and unprepared, one arriving calm and with written notes — conveying the intake quality difference]

---

**5A — Identity Anchor:**

Here's what I want you to take away from this.

Pipeline health isn't about whether you had a good month. It's about whether the four systems that feed your pipeline are structurally sound or structurally vulnerable. And most firms that feel like their growth has stalled — not failing, just not growing — have at least two of these four vital signs in the yellow or red.

The firm that's going to be in a significantly better position 18 months from now is the one that runs this diagnostic today and starts addressing the right problem — not more marketing activity on top of a pipeline that has a structural issue underneath it.

**5B — CTA:**

If you want it, DM me the word DIAGNOSTIC — one word — and I'll send it to you directly. No call required. You can run it on your own, see where you stand, and decide from there what you want to do about it.

The picture is usually pretty clear. What I want to walk through is the specific lever that makes the bridge between where you are and that picture — and why it's almost always the one lever that's been underestimated. ## VALUE BOMB VARIANT — VIDEO 5

**Title:** 4 Vital Signs — Family Law Pipeline Health Check (4-Minute Version)

Four things. If you know where you stand on all four, you know exactly where your pipeline problem is.

Vital Sign One: Source concentration. More than 70% of your revenue coming from fewer than three sources or a single channel is a structural vulnerability. It doesn't feel dangerous until it is. Run the audit — 30 minutes, a spreadsheet of the last 24 months of matters. Calculate your concentration map. If any single source is above 25%, you have work to do.

Vital Sign Two: Intake speed. The conversion rate difference between a 30-minute response and a 24-hour response is over 50% in most documented studies. That's not a marketing problem — that's an operational problem. Measure your last 30 consultation requests. First contact to first response, in hours. If that average is above four hours on business days, that's your leak.

Vital Sign Three: Revenue per matter by case type versus case type distribution. If the case type that produces the most revenue per matter is also the minority of your new matters, your marketing and your positioning are misaligned. You're attracting the cases easiest to explain, not the cases you most want to win.

Vital Sign Four: Content-sourced intake. What percentage of new matters in the last 12 months came from content you produced? If the answer is zero, you are 100% dependent on channels you don't control. That's the ceiling.

Four vital signs. One hour of honest measurement. Run it this week.

DM me DIAGNOSTIC for the full assessment framework.

## CONTRARIAN VARIANT — VIDEO 5

**Title:** Stop Trying to Fix Your Pipeline. You Don't Know What's Broken Yet.

Here's the problem with most law firm marketing conversations: they start with the solution.

"We need more referrals." So let's build a referral program.
"We need more online visibility." So let's do SEO.
"We need more consultations." So let's run ads.

None of those are wrong answers. They're just answers to questions nobody asked yet.

The question nobody asks is: where exactly is the pipeline failing? Because "the pipeline isn't growing" is not a diagnosis. It's a symptom. And you can throw a significant amount of money at the wrong solution and make exactly zero progress — because you were treating a source concentration problem with SEO, or a positioning problem with a referral program, or an intake speed problem with more content.

I've run the same four-point diagnostic on dozens of family law firms. Here's what I've found: the presenting complaint is almost never the actual problem. Managing partners who come to me convinced they need better SEO often have a source concentration problem. Firms convinced they need a referral strategy often have an intake speed problem that's leaking the referrals they're already getting.

The fix starts with the diagnostic, not the solution.

Four vital signs. Source concentration. Intake speed. Revenue per matter alignment. Content-sourced intake percentage. Those four numbers tell you almost everything you need to know about where the constraint is.

Measure first. Then fix.

DM me DIAGNOSTIC and I'll send you the one-page framework.

# VIDEO 6 — WHAT DOES YOUR IDEAL LAW FIRM ACTUALLY LOOK LIKE IN 5 YEARS?

```
VIDEO 6 — What Does Your Ideal Law Firm Actually Look Like in 5 Years?
Mirror Post: Day 8 — "Most managing partners can sketch that picture clearly. They know exactly what it looks like. The gap isn't vision — it's the belief that there's a system that can actually get them there."
Stage: B3/E3
Pillar: Practical Tool
Target Length: 9–11 min
Dopamine Driver: Aspiration + Blueprint
Thumbnail Angle: Roger with a simple drawn bridge on a whiteboard behind him — labeled "NOW" on the left side and "FIVE YEARS" on the right; text overlay "THE BRIDGE MOST FIRMS NEVER BUILD"
```

---

## SECTION 1 — HOOK (0:00–0:45)

**Primary Hook:**

Most managing partners I talk to know exactly what they want their practice to look like in five years. The case mix, the client type, the referral relationships, the revenue. The picture is clear. What's missing isn't the vision — it's the bridge. The system that takes you from where the practice is today to that picture. This video is about building that bridge. Specifically, the one lever that almost every family law firm underestimates.

[B-ROLL: A managing partner looking out a window — thoughtful, not distressed — conveying the clarity of vision and the weight of distance]

**Alt Hook A (for returning subscribers):**

This week we've been walking through the full diagnostic picture — the decision sequence, the pipeline vital signs. Today I want to zoom out. Because all of that information is useful only if it's pointed at something. And the something I want to talk about is the five-year picture — and specifically, the bridge that most family law firms never actually build between where they are and where they want to be.

**Alt Hook B (pattern-interrupt):**

You've probably heard the advice: start with the vision. Work backward. Set goals, build the plan. And it's good advice. The problem is that most family law marketing programs don't actually connect to the five-year picture. They connect to last quarter's results. And when the program doesn't produce the results, the vision gets quietly revised downward instead of the program getting replaced.

**The Promise:**

I'm Roger with Legal Growth Dynamics. We help family law managing partners build that bridge — not with ads or generic SEO, but with an authority content system designed specifically for the way high-value family law clients actually make decisions.

---

## SECTION 2 — SETUP / PROBLEM FRAME (0:45–2:00)

Let me describe the five-year picture that most managing partners I talk to share. Not all of them — but a strong majority.

They want predictable consultation flow. Not waiting for the phone to ring, not sweating about what next month looks like, but a steady, manageable volume of qualified inquiries from people they actually want to work with. They want a better case mix — more of the complex, high-value matters and fewer of the high-volume, low-margin cases that consume staff capacity and generate limited revenue per hour of attorney time. They want credible referral relationships — not just a few loyal referral sources who've been sending cases for years, but a broader network of professionals who think of them specifically when the right situation arises. And they want revenue that reflects the quality of the work — not just more revenue, but revenue that comes with some predictability and reflects real positioning.

[B-ROLL: Managing partner in a well-organized conference room, looking comfortable and in control — conveying the aspirational state, not the current one]

That picture is almost universally clear. I've never met a managing partner who couldn't describe it. What they struggle to describe is the path — specifically, what they would need to change in the next 12 to 18 months to be meaningfully closer to that picture.

And when I ask them what they've tried — what systems or programs they've invested in with the intention of moving toward that picture — the answers are almost always the same. They've run ads. They've done SEO. They've tried a content marketing agency. They've redone their website. And the results ranged from marginal to disappointing.

[B-ROLL: A managing partner gesturing through a list on a whiteboard — multiple checked boxes but no clear forward movement — conveying the effort without traction]

Not because those things are inherently wrong. But because none of them were connected to the picture. They were standalone tactics with standalone metrics. And a collection of tactics without a coherent system is not a bridge. It's a series of disconnected planks that don't reach the other side.

---

## SECTION 3 — THE TURN (2:00–3:30)

Here's the shift I want to offer.

The five-year picture most managing partners have always includes — without exception — a version of being known as the authority for something specific. The go-to firm for high-asset divorce in their market. The firm that handles complex custody modifications. The attorneys that business-owning clients trust when a marriage ends and significant assets are at stake.

The picture always includes being known. It just rarely includes a deliberate plan for building that recognition.

[B-ROLL: Two attorney profiles side by side — one generic with stock photo and broad practice description, one specific with professional video thumbnail, clear niche focus, and notable engagement numbers]

That's the one lever I mentioned at the top of this video. Authority positioning — the deliberate, systematic building of public recognition for specific expertise with specific clients — is consistently the most underestimated lever in the five-year pictures I hear.

And I understand why. Because authority positioning is slow. It's a 12 to 18 month project before it compounds in ways that feel material. It doesn't produce a spike in consultations the week you start. And it requires a kind of consistency that most content marketing programs — the ones that promise results in 90 days — are not built to deliver.

[B-ROLL: A slowly growing plant in time-lapse — conveying the compounding nature of something built over time rather than a quick spike]

But here's the counterpoint: the firms that started building it 18 months ago are the ones with waiting lists right now. It's not magic. It's compounding. And the only way to not benefit from it in 18 months is to not start it now.

---

## SECTION 4 — THE PROOF / MECHANISM (3:30–7:00)

[CHAPTER: What Authority Content Actually Does (~3:30)]

Let me make this concrete. When I say authority content system, I mean something specific — not a blog post strategy, not a social media calendar, not a video series in isolation.

An authority content system has three functions operating simultaneously. The first function is positioning — content that articulates, publicly and specifically, what you're the expert for and why it matters to your ideal client. Not "we handle all family law matters." The exact opposite of that. Content that says "here's what I know about high-asset divorce that most attorneys don't understand — and here's why that matters to you."

[B-ROLL: A specific YouTube thumbnail for a high-asset divorce video — not generic; the title is precise and targeted]

The second function is presence — the consistent accumulation of content that means a prospect researching family law attorneys in your market will encounter you multiple times, across multiple formats, before they ever speak to anyone at your firm. Presence is not about volume. It's about coverage across the decision journey we talked about on Monday — Stages One through Six. If you're present at every stage, the firm that only shows up at Stage Five is at a structural disadvantage.

[B-ROLL: A prospect's research journey visualized — different video thumbnails, article screenshots, LinkedIn posts — all from the same attorney, encountered at different points in a 30-day research window]

The third function is compounding. Unlike paid ads — which stop the moment you stop paying — content accumulates authority over time. A video that was produced 14 months ago and still appears in search results when someone in your market researches high-asset divorce attorneys is producing results without requiring any additional investment. That compounding effect is what makes authority content categorically different from any paid channel.

[CHAPTER: The Difference Between Presence and Authority (~5:00)]

Here's a distinction that matters and that most attorneys conflate.

Presence means you're visible. You have content out there. People who look for you can find you. That's not nothing — but it's also not authority.

Authority means that people who weren't looking for you specifically encounter you in the course of doing research, find your content more credible, more specific, and more useful than anyone else's, and arrive at the consultation already predisposed to hire you. That's a completely different dynamic. And it requires a completely different kind of content.

[B-ROLL: Two intake calls — one from a prospect who found the firm via a Google ad and is price-shopping; one from a prospect who found the firm via a specific video series and says "I've been watching your videos for a few weeks — I feel like I already know how you think about this"]

[PACING NOTE: Slow here. The distinction between presence and authority is the conceptual heart of this video. Don't rush past it.]

Presence content is typically broad. It covers general topics because the goal is to be found. Authority content is deliberately narrow. It goes deep on the specific situation your ideal client is facing — so deep that the ideal client thinks "this is exactly what I needed to understand" and the wrong client self-selects out because the content isn't for them.

That narrowness is counterintuitive. Most attorneys resist it because it feels like you're excluding potential clients. What it actually does is dramatically increase the quality of the clients you attract — because the content is speaking directly to the person you most want to represent.

[CHAPTER: The First 90 Days of the Bridge (~6:00)]

Let me give you the practical starting point. Because the five-year picture is useful, but what you need this week is a concrete next step.

The first thing to build before anything else is your positioning statement. Not a tagline, not a mission statement — a single clear articulation of who specifically you serve, what specific problem you solve for them, and why you're the credible choice. That statement is the architectural frame for everything else. Without it, content is random. With it, content has direction.

[B-ROLL: Simple whiteboard with three columns labeled "Who," "What Problem," "Why You" — being filled in with specific family law language]

The second thing to build is your content foundation — a set of three to five cornerstone pieces of content that cover the most important questions your ideal client asks at each stage of the decision journey. Not ten pieces, not a full calendar. Three to five deep, specific, authoritative pieces that can be referenced, updated, and repurposed. These are the pillars that everything else builds on.

The third thing to build — and this one surprises people — is your referral intelligence layer. A map of the 15 to 20 professional relationships in your market that most directly influence the type of client you want to attract. Not a referral program. A deliberate understanding of who influences your ideal client's decision and what they need to see from you to refer confidently. Your content is the tool that activates those relationships — because content gives your referral sources something specific to point to when they recommend you.

At 30 days, you have your positioning statement and your content foundation underway. At 60 days, the cornerstone content is published and you've identified your referral intelligence targets. At 90 days, you're producing consistently against your positioning and your referral sources are beginning to encounter your content in their own research and social feeds.

That's the first 90 days of the bridge.

[B-ROLL: Simple calendar visual — 30, 60, 90 day markers with brief text descriptions of milestones — not a full calendar, just the arc]

---

## SECTION 5 — THE CLOSE (7:00–END)

**5A — Identity Anchor:**

You have the picture. You've always had the picture. What you haven't had — until recently — is the specific framework for what to build first, in what order, with what purpose. Most of what's been sold to family law managing partners as "marketing" has been presence without architecture. Activity without positioning.

The firms that will look exactly like the five-year picture 18 months from now are the ones that start building the bridge today — with clarity about what it's actually made of.

**5B — CTA:**

If you want to see where your bridge is actually broken — which of the three components is the constraint — the fastest way to find out is through the diagnostic at survey.LegalGrowthDynamics.com. It takes about five minutes. The questions are specific to family law. And the output gives you a clear read on whether your constraint is positioning, presence, or referral intelligence — and what to do about it.

You can also DM me BRIDGE and I'll send you the 90-day starting framework directly. Either way, the next step is yours.

**5C — Channel Hook:**

Tomorrow we're talking about market identity — specifically, what your firm's name actually means in your market right now and how to build that meaning deliberately. If you've ever wondered why certain attorneys are always the first name that comes up in referral conversations and others aren't — even when the work quality is comparable — that video answers the question. See you tomorrow.

## VALUE BOMB VARIANT — VIDEO 6

**Title:** The 90-Day Bridge — What to Build First If You Want a Different Practice in 18 Months

Most law firm marketing starts in the middle. Someone decides to do content, or SEO, or referral development — and starts executing without the architectural layer underneath it.

Here's what the architectural layer consists of:

First: a positioning statement. Not a tagline. A single clear answer to three questions — who specifically you serve, what specific problem you solve, and why you're credibly the right choice. This is not a marketing exercise. It's a strategic decision. And without it, every piece of content you produce is a guess.

Second: a content foundation. Three to five cornerstone pieces — deep, specific, authoritative — that cover the most important questions your ideal client asks before they're ready to call someone. These are not blog posts. They are the definitive resource for anyone researching your specific area of focus in your market. Produce these before you produce anything else.

Third: referral intelligence. A deliberate map of the 15 to 20 professional relationships in your market that most influence the type of client you want. Not a referral outreach program — a strategic picture of who talks to your ideal client before they're ready to hire an attorney, and what those people need to see from you to refer with confidence. Your content is the tool that builds those relationships at scale.

Those three things, built in that order, in 90 days. That's the bridge.

Everything else — the calendar volume, the social strategy, the ad layer — comes after the architecture is in place.

DM me BRIDGE for the 90-day starting framework.

---

## CONTRARIAN VARIANT — VIDEO 6

**Title:** Your Five-Year Vision Is Probably Working Against You

Every managing partner has a five-year vision. Most of them have the same one: more of the right cases, less of the wrong cases, predictable pipeline, better revenue.

That vision is not wrong. But it's also not useful — because it's not distinguishing. Every attorney in your market wants the same thing. And a goal you share with everyone you're competing against is not a strategy.

Here's the reframe.

The five-year picture needs one specific element that differentiates it: the specific thing you become known for. Not "quality representation" — every attorney says that. Not "personalized service" — same problem. The specific case type, the specific client profile, the specific area of expertise where your name comes up first in the relevant professional conversations in your market.

That's the center of gravity the five-year vision is missing. Because without a specific positioning center, you build content for everyone and reach no one with enough specificity to be memorable. You build referral relationships that are generally positive but not specifically activated. You show up in searches but not as the clear choice for the client who most needs to find you.

The vision doesn't need to be bigger. It needs to be more specific.

What is the one case type, the one client situation, the one professional context where your firm should be the first name anyone in your market thinks of?

Start there. Build the bridge from that point outward.

DM me BRIDGE and I'll send you the 90-day framework for building from a specific position.

# VIDEO 7 — WHAT'S YOUR LAW FIRM'S NAME IN THE MARKET? (AND HOW TO BUILD IT DELIBERATELY)

```
VIDEO 7 — What's Your Law Firm's Name in the Market? (And How to Build It Deliberately)
Mirror Post: Day 9 — "What is your firm's name in the market? Not the legal name on your letterhead. Your market name — what your ideal client associates with your firm before they call anyone."
Stage: B3/E3
Pillar: Practical Tool
Target Length: 10–11 min
Dopamine Driver: Identity + Self-Audit + Mastery
Thumbnail Angle: Roger looking directly at camera, slightly leaning in, with the text "WHAT'S YOUR MARKET NAME?" in large letters and a small subline "It's not what's on your letterhead"
```

---

## SECTION 1 — HOOK (0:00–0:45)

**Primary Hook:**

Here's a question I want you to sit with: if three CPAs in your city were asked, right now, to name the best family law attorney for a business-owning client facing a high-asset divorce — would your name come up? For most attorneys, the honest answer is no. Not because they're not good at the work. Because they haven't established a specific market identity with the people who make referrals. Today I want to show you what that market identity is, how it's built, and why it's the difference between a predictable pipeline and waiting for the phone to ring.

[B-ROLL: A CPA's desk — referral phone call scenario where they're asked for an attorney recommendation and they pause, uncertain — conveying the cost of a missing market name]

**Alt Hook A (for returning subscribers):**

This week we've been building toward a specific question, and today is the day we ask it directly: what does your firm's name actually mean in your market? Not what you want it to mean. What it currently means — to the referral sources, the professional networks, the potential clients who are forming impressions of you right now based on what they can find and what they've heard. Because the gap between what you want your name to mean and what it currently means is the gap this video is about.

**Alt Hook B (data-led / contrarian):**

Most attorneys assume that if they're known for quality work, referrals will follow. And they're partially right. But "quality work" is not a market identity. It's a baseline expectation. The attorney who becomes the first name in a referral conversation isn't just the one who does good work — they're the one who has made their expertise specifically, publicly, and repeatedly clear to the people who make referrals. That's a different thing. And almost no one is deliberately building it.

**The Promise:**

I'm Roger with Legal Growth Dynamics. We build specific market identities for family law firms — through content that positions you for the right clients before anyone searches. This video explains the framework.

---

## SECTION 2 — SETUP / PROBLEM FRAME (0:45–2:00)

Let me tell you what happens in a referral conversation — not the version you're told about, but what actually happens.

A CPA has a client going through a divorce. It's complex — there's a business involved, there are retirement accounts, there's some real estate. The client asks the CPA: "Do you know a good family law attorney?" The CPA thinks for a moment. One or two names come to mind. Not because they've done extensive research. Because those names have a specific association in the CPA's mind — a specific reason to recommend them for this specific type of situation. One attorney's name comes up because the CPA saw three of their LinkedIn posts about business valuation in divorce over the last six months. Another name comes up because they sponsored the local accounting association's last event. Your name doesn't come up — not because you can't do the work, but because there's no specific association in that CPA's mind that makes you the obvious choice for this situation.

[B-ROLL: A brief animated visualization of referral associations — certain attorneys with clear labels ("business valuation," "high-asset," "complex custody") and others with no label or a generic one ("family law - general")]

That's the market name problem. And it's not a problem of reputation in the general sense. Your general reputation may be excellent. The problem is that general reputation doesn't activate referrals in specific situations — specific positioning does.

Most family law attorneys have invested heavily in being known as good. Very few have invested in being known as the specific expert for the specific thing their best clients most need.

[B-ROLL: Two side-by-side Google search results for "family law attorney [city]" — one firm with broad generic descriptions, one with specific language about high-asset divorce and business owner representation — conveying the clarity difference]

And because that specific positioning hasn't been built deliberately — through content, through specific language, through consistent public articulation — the referral sources in your market are filling in the blank themselves. Which usually means they're defaulting to whoever showed up most recently in their feed. Or whoever sponsored something. Or whoever a mutual colleague mentioned. It's not strategic on their end because it can't be. The signal isn't there.

---

## SECTION 3 — THE TURN (2:00–3:30)

Here's the reframe I want to offer on market identity — and it's a simple one, but it changes everything about how you approach your marketing.

Your market name is not a branding exercise. It's not a tagline or a color palette or a website redesign. It's the specific answer to one question in the mind of everyone in your professional network who has the ability to refer your ideal client to you: "Who should I call for this situation?"

[B-ROLL: The single question "Who should I call for this situation?" written on a whiteboard or displayed on screen — simple, direct]

When that question arises — and it arises every week in the professional networks around your market — there are a limited number of names that come up. The attorneys whose names come up consistently have something in common: they have made their specific expertise known, repeatedly, in formats and channels their referral sources actually encounter. Not because they had a better website. Because they were present in the conversation — through content, through events, through specific language — that made their expertise unmistakably clear.

That's a buildable thing. It's not mysterious. It doesn't require a large budget. It requires clarity about what you want to be known for — and a consistent, deliberate effort to make that known in the right places to the right people.

[B-ROLL: An attorney recording a LinkedIn video — not in a studio, just professional and specific — conveying that this is accessible and deliberate, not glamorous]

The attorneys who have strong market identities didn't get them accidentally. Someone made a decision — consciously or not — about what the firm was going to stand for, and they've been consistent about it. Name Yourself Day — the hook for today — is exactly the right frame for this. Because naming yourself deliberately is the difference between having a market identity that works for you and having an absence of identity that leaves the market to fill in the blank.

---

## SECTION 4 — THE PROOF / MECHANISM (3:30–7:00)

[CHAPTER: The Three Types of Market Positioning in Family Law (~3:30)]

In the markets where we work, I've observed that family law attorneys tend to cluster into one of three positioning types. Understanding which one you're in right now — and which one you want to move toward — clarifies everything about what to produce and where to show up.

The first positioning type is generalist. This is the firm that handles all family law matters — divorce, custody, adoption, protective orders, modifications, everything. The marketing message is "we're here when you need us." The referral activation is low because there's no specific trigger. When a CPA has a complex business divorce, a generalist firm doesn't necessarily come to mind because nothing in the firm's messaging signals specialized expertise for that situation.

[B-ROLL: Generic family law website homepage — stock photos, "We Handle All Family Law Matters" header, long list of practice areas]

The second positioning type is matter-specific. This is the firm that is known for a specific type of case — high-asset divorce, military divorce, complex custody, international family law. The marketing is more targeted, the referral activation is stronger because there's a clear trigger, and the case mix tends to reflect the positioning over time. This is the most common aspirational positioning for managing partners who want to move away from generalist practice.

The third positioning type — and this is the least common and the highest value — is client-specific. This is the firm that is known for representing a specific type of client, regardless of the exact case type. The firm that represents business owners in family law matters. The firm that handles family law for high-net-worth individuals. The firm that specializes in the family law issues that arise in professional partnerships and medical practices. Client-specific positioning is harder to build because it requires more nuanced content and a more deliberate referral strategy — but it produces dramatically stronger referral activation because the trigger is embedded in the client description, not just the case type.

[B-ROLL: Attorney website showing specific language: "We represent business-owning clients facing divorce — protecting what you've built while navigating what you're facing" — conveying the specificity of client-first positioning]

[PACING NOTE: Pause after each positioning type. Give the viewer time to locate themselves. Many will immediately recognize which category they're in.]

[CHAPTER: How a Market Name Gets Built (~5:00)]

The market name is built through three mechanisms — and all three have to be active simultaneously to compound.

The first mechanism is content. Specifically, public content that articulates your positioning in terms your referral sources and ideal clients will recognize and remember. Not "we handle complex divorces" — that's still generic. "Here's what most attorneys don't understand about business valuation in high-asset divorce — and why it costs clients money when their attorney doesn't get this right." That's specific. That's memorable. That's the kind of content that a CPA will tag and send to a colleague because it's useful and specific enough to be relevant.

The second mechanism is consistency. One good video doesn't build a market name. Neither does one good article. A market name is built by showing up with the same specific expertise, in the same specific voice, across the same specific platforms, over a period of time that most people give up before reaching. The research I've done suggests that meaningful market identity recognition — the point where a referral source thinks of you specifically when the right situation arises — typically requires somewhere between nine and fourteen months of consistent presence. That's not discouraging. It's actually clarifying. Because it means the firms that are willing to stay consistent for a year are almost automatically differentiating themselves from the ones who quit after three months.

[B-ROLL: Calendar visual — months labeled, with a consistent output pattern shown as a line that builds over time — then a marker at the 9–12 month range labeled "Recognition Threshold"]

The third mechanism is specificity of language. Your market name is built through the specific words you use repeatedly in your content, your speaking, your referral conversations. Not just "divorce attorney" or "family law firm" — but "the firm that protects what business owners have built when a marriage ends." When referral sources hear that language repeatedly, it becomes the trigger. When someone says "I know a business owner going through a divorce," your name comes up because the trigger matches the positioning language they've encountered from you.

[CHAPTER: Naming Yourself Deliberately (~6:15)]

Here's the practical exercise. Two questions that define your market positioning statement.

Question One: What is the specific client situation — the precise combination of client type and case complexity — that you are most equipped, most experienced, and most interested in representing? Not the broadest possible answer. The most specific true answer. If you could fill your calendar with one type of matter from one type of client, what would it be?

Question Two: What do you understand about that client's situation that most attorneys — and most of their marketing — misses or understates? What is the specific insight or approach that makes you the right choice for that person rather than the firm down the street?

[B-ROLL: Roger at a desk with a notepad, writing — conveying the working-through of these questions in real time rather than presenting a polished answer]

Your positioning statement is the intersection of those two answers. It names who you serve, what you understand about their situation, and why that understanding makes you the credible choice. That statement becomes the foundation of your content, your referral conversations, and your digital presence.

The first three pieces of content to produce — once you've named your position — are designed to make that positioning unmistakably public. The first piece articulates the specific problem your ideal client faces in their own language. The second piece demonstrates a specific aspect of your expertise that differentiates your approach. The third piece shows a client situation — with enough specificity to be recognizable — where your approach produced a meaningfully different outcome than a generalist approach would have.

Those three pieces are not just content. They are the public articulation of your market name. They're what a referral source will encounter and forward. They're what a prospect will find and return to. They're what begins to make "who should I call for this?" answerable with your name.

---

## SECTION 5 — THE CLOSE (7:00–END)

**5A — Identity Anchor:**

Your market name already exists. Right now, in the professional networks around your practice, there is some version of what people associate with your firm's name. The question is whether that association is the one you would have chosen deliberately — or the one that formed by default.

The attorneys who fill their calendars with the cases they most want to represent didn't get there by accident. They named themselves deliberately. They chose the specific client, the specific situation, the specific expertise. And then they built content and relationships that made that choice visible and credible to everyone who needed to know.

That's a buildable thing. It starts with two questions and three content pieces. And it compounds for as long as you stay consistent.

**5B — CTA:**

If you want the framework for identifying your positioning statement — the specific questions we walk every family law firm through before we produce a single piece of content — DM me the word NAME. One word. I'll send you the positioning framework directly so you can work through it for your own firm.

DM me NAME.

**5C — Channel Hook:**

Tomorrow is the last video of this week, and it might be the most direct one. I'm going to walk you through why two equally skilled attorneys in the same market can have completely different pipelines — and why the difference is almost never what most people assume. If you've ever felt like you're doing everything right and still not growing the way you should be, that video is for you. See you tomorrow.

## VALUE BOMB VARIANT — VIDEO 7

**Title:** Two Questions That Define Your Law Firm's Market Name

Most attorneys can describe their practice. Very few can name their market position.

Here's the difference: describing your practice is what you put on your website. Naming your market position is what your referral sources say when someone asks for a recommendation.

The gap between those two things is built with two questions.

Question One: What is the specific client situation — the exact combination of client type and case complexity — where you are most equipped, most experienced, and most genuinely interested in the work? Not the broadest answer. The most specific true answer. If you could fill your calendar exclusively with one type of matter from one type of client, what would that be?

Question Two: What do you understand about that situation that most attorneys don't address in their marketing? Not just that you're experienced — what specific knowledge, approach, or perspective differentiates how you handle that situation versus a generalist?

The intersection of those two answers is your market positioning statement. It's the sentence that should drive your content, your referral conversations, and your digital presence. It's the sentence that — when a CPA hears it three times in three months across three different formats — becomes the trigger that puts your name in that referral conversation.

Most attorneys have never articulated it. Which means their market name is being built by default — by whatever impression people have formed in the absence of a deliberate one.

Name yourself deliberately. Start with those two questions.

DM me NAME for the full positioning framework.

## CONTRARIAN VARIANT — VIDEO 7

**Title:** Your Law Firm's Brand Is Not What You Think It Is

Let me tell you what most managing partners think their firm's brand is.

Their logo. Their website color scheme. Their tagline. Their Google reviews average. Possibly the fact that they sponsor the local bar association's annual gala.

None of that is your brand. Not in any way that matters to the referral source who's about to recommend you — or not recommend you — to their high-value client.

Your brand — your actual market brand — is the specific association your name triggers in the minds of the professionals who refer your ideal clients. That's it. Not your logo, not your website. The association.

And for most family law firms, if you were able to audit that association honestly, you'd find one of two things. Either you have a general positive association — "good firm, good attorneys, been around a while" — that doesn't activate specific referrals because it doesn't have a specific trigger. Or you have no particular association at all, and your referral sources like you personally but can't identify why they'd send a specific type of client to you over anyone else.

Neither of those is a brand in any useful sense. A useful brand is a specific trigger. When someone says "I know a business owner going through a divorce," a specific attorney's name comes up because that attorney has made their expertise for that exact situation unmistakably clear — repeatedly, publicly, over time.

That's what a market name actually is. And it's built with content and consistency, not logos and taglines.

The question isn't "what's our brand?" The question is "what association have we built in the minds of the people who refer our ideal clients?" And if the answer isn't specific, it isn't working.

DM me NAME. I'll send you the positioning framework.
